```markdown
cssclasses: []
notes: "All links below are selected for lab-style interactivity (choices, reaction times, scores, or interpretable outputs). Use neutral framing and avoid personal disclosure."
---

## Selection rationale
- These activities are free to run and do not require paid courseware licenses. PsyToolkit explicitly states it is “totally free.” citeturn12search11
- These activities function like online labs because students generate interpretable outputs (choices, reaction times, errors, or scored scales). PsyToolkit also notes students can run demos in-browser and copy response-time/error data. citeturn3search4turn1search0
- They align cleanly to Intro Psych motivation themes: incentives and reward pursuit, risk–reward tradeoffs, approach–avoidance, self-regulation, and needs.

## Core interactive lab tasks (behavioral tasks with observable performance)

### Incentives and reward learning (choice patterns over time)
- PsyToolkit: Iowa Gambling Task (IGT) overview: https://www.psytoolkit.org/experiment-library/igt.html citeturn0search0
- PsyToolkit: IGT run page: https://www.psytoolkit.org/experiment-library/experiment_igt.html citeturn0search4

Teaching angle
- Treat as “reward chasing vs long-term payoff.” Debrief why people persist with high-reward/high-loss decks and how feedback shapes behavior.

Suggested student data capture
- Count choices from each deck in the last half of the task, or record the final score shown on-screen (if displayed).

### Risk-taking for reward (stop vs keep going under uncertainty)
- PsyToolkit: Balloon Analogue Risk Task (BART) overview: https://www.psytoolkit.org/experiment-library/bart.html citeturn3search2
- PsyToolkit: BART run page: https://www.psytoolkit.org/experiment-library/experiment_bart.html citeturn3search5

Teaching angle
- Clean demonstration of incentive motivation: each pump increases reward but raises risk.

Suggested student data capture
- Average pumps on non-explosion trials (if shown), number of explosions, or final points.

### Intertemporal choice (delay discounting: “now vs later”)
- Pavlovia demo: delay_discount run page: https://run.pavlovia.org/demos/delay_discount/ citeturn10search1
- Description page (task summary): https://gitlab.pavlovia.org/demos/delay_discount citeturn11search0

Teaching angle
- Make “delay discounting” concrete with repeated choices between smaller-sooner and larger-later rewards.

Suggested student data capture
- Proportion of “immediate” choices across the task, or the smallest immediate amount a student accepts instead of a delayed option (if the task displays it).

### Approach–avoidance tendencies (motivational orientation measured as reaction time)
- PsyToolkit: VAAST with words overview: https://www.psytoolkit.org/experiment-library/vaast_words.html citeturn13search1
- PsyToolkit: VAAST with words run page: https://www.psytoolkit.org/experiment-library/experiment_vaast_words.html citeturn13search0
- PsyToolkit: VAAST with images overview: https://www.psytoolkit.org/experiment-library/vaast_images.html citeturn12search2
- PsyToolkit: VAAST with images run page: https://www.psytoolkit.org/experiment-library/experiment_vaast_images.html citeturn12search1

Teaching angle
- Links motivation to action tendencies: approach positive stimuli faster than negative, and avoid negative faster than positive (compatibility effect).

Suggested student data capture
- Compatibility effect as a single number: mean RT incompatible minus mean RT compatible (if the task displays summary stats; otherwise screenshot final feedback).

## Motivation-adjacent measurement labs (scored scales with strong classroom debrief value)

### Needs and need satisfaction (theory-to-measurement bridge)
- PsyToolkit: Maslow’s Hierarchy of Needs questionnaire (includes “Run the demo”): https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/maslow.html citeturn3search3

Teaching angle
- Students see how “big theory” becomes measurable subscales and why measurement choices matter.

Suggested student data capture
- One subscale score plus one interpretation sentence: “Which need domain looks least satisfied and why might that be context-dependent?”

### Persistence toward long-term goals (self-regulatory motivation)
- PsyToolkit: Short Grit Scale (Grit-S): https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/grit-short.html citeturn2search0

Teaching angle
- Use as a measurement lab: discuss traits vs states, reliability, and what grit does and does not predict.

Suggested student data capture
- Total grit score and one “measurement limitation” note.

### Procrastination as motivational conflict (avoidance, self-handicapping, task aversion)
- PsyToolkit: General Procrastination Scale (GP): https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/procrastination-gp.html citeturn2search16

Teaching angle
- Map procrastination onto immediate reinforcement, aversive tasks, and emotion regulation without turning it into a confessional assignment.

Suggested student data capture
- Score plus one plausible confound (sleep, stress, workload week).

### Impulsiveness (reward sensitivity and control problems as measurable tendencies)
- PsyToolkit: Barratt Impulsiveness Scale (BIS): https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/impulsiveness-barratt.html citeturn2search3

Teaching angle
- Connect impulsiveness to delay discounting and risk tasks: prediction before data.

Suggested student data capture
- BIS score and a hypothesis: “Higher BIS predicts more immediate choices / more pumps.”

### Risk propensity (short, fast, good predictor variable for BART/IGT)
- PsyToolkit: Risk Propensity Scale (RPS): https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/risk-rps.html citeturn3search20

Teaching angle
- Use as the “predictor variable” in a simple correlational class dataset.

Suggested student data capture
- RPS score + BART metric, later graphed in Topic 4.

## Teaching designs that feel like real online labs

### Option A: 50-minute station rotation (high engagement, low tech risk)
- Station 1: BART (risk–reward)
- Station 2: Delay discounting (now vs later)
- Station 3: VAAST (approach–avoidance)
- Station 4: One scale (RPS or BIS) to use as a predictor variable
- Whole-class debrief: “What did we operationalize as motivation in each station?”

Grading-friendly submission
- One numeric output per station plus one screenshot (or one copied score line) to reduce disputes about data entry.

### Option B: Hypothesis-first mini-study (teaches scientific reasoning inside the content)
- Pre-register in class (informally): “Higher impulsiveness predicts more immediate choices and more risk-taking.”
- Collect: BIS score + delay discounting proportion-immediate + BART risk metric.
- Debrief: Does the pattern support the hypothesis? What would you change in a real study?

### Debrief prompts that force lab thinking
- What is the independent variable in this activity (or what differs across trials)?
- What is the dependent variable and how is it quantified (RT, choices, points, score)?
- What threatens validity here (device differences, distractions, misunderstanding instructions, time pressure)?
- What alternative explanation competes with the motivation explanation (learning, fatigue, comprehension, speed–accuracy tradeoff)?

## Practical cautions (keeps this topic academically safe)
- Avoid requiring students to disclose personal financial stress, substance use, or mental health details.
- Use neutral phrasing: “This task demonstrates choice under incentives,” not “This reveals what you are like.”
- Encourage anonymous IDs if you plan to show class distributions.
```

### Block 44 (lang=markdown | source=md | block 44)

```markdown
---
title: "PSY-132 Interactive Teaching Labs (Free Only): Emotion"
filename: "psy-132_topic-18_emotion_interactive-teaching-labs_free"
type: "resource_links"
status: "curated"
created: 2025-12-31
updated: 2025-12-31
origin: "Curated for PSY-132 Topic 18 with strict emphasis on free, student-facing interactive lab activities (tasks, scored measures, and decision-based interactives)."
citation_style: "APA"
verification_needed: false
tags:
  - psy-132
  - topic-18
  - emotion
  - interactive-labs
  - free
topics:
  - "PSY-132"
  - "Emotion"
aliases:
  - "Topic 18 emotion labs"
sources:
  - "https://www.psytoolkit.org/experiment-library/"
  - "https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/"
  - "https://labinthewild.org/"
cssclasses: []
notes: "All activities below support lab-style teaching: students generate interpretable outputs (reaction time, accuracy, choices, or scores). Use neutral framing and avoid personal disclosure requirements."
---

## Selection rationale
- Free access for students.
- Browser-run interactivity (tasks, scored measures, or decision/recognition activities).
- Outputs are easy to capture for grading and debrief (one number, one screenshot, one brief interpretation).

## Core lab hubs (reusable all semester)
- PsyToolkit experiment library (run browser demos): https://www.psytoolkit.org/experiment-library/
- PsyToolkit survey library (run scored scales with feedback): https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/
- Lab in the Wild (short web studies with immediate feedback): https://labinthewild.org/

## Lab set A: Emotion and attention (behavioral task; reaction time and accuracy)

### Attentional bias / threat monitoring (Dot-Probe)
- PsyToolkit Dot-Probe overview: https://www.psytoolkit.org/experiment-library/dotprobe.html
- PsyToolkit Dot-Probe run page: https://www.psytoolkit.org/experiment-library/experiment_dotprobe.html

Teaching use
- Clean demonstration that emotionally salient stimuli can capture attention.
- Debrief the operational definition: “bias” as faster responses when the probe follows a threat-related word.

Student data capture (choose one)
- Mean reaction time by condition (if shown).
- A screenshot of the results screen plus one sentence: “Which condition was faster?”

## Lab set B: Emotion recognition and labeling (performance output; debriefable)

### Read emotion from eyes (classic “social emotion perception” performance task)
- Lab in the Wild: Social Intelligence Test (emotion-from-eyes): https://socialintelligence.labinthewild.org/

### Emotion recognition from full faces (photo-based recognition; scored output)
- Greater Good Science Center: Emotional Intelligence Quiz (identify emotion in photos): https://greatergood.berkeley.edu/quizzes/ei_quiz
- Greater Good embedded quiz link (same quiz, simpler embed): https://greatergood.berkeley.edu/quizzes/embed/ei_quiz

### Micro-expression recognition (very fast exposures; quiz output)
- Paul Ekman Group: Micro Expressions Test (free): https://www.paulekman.com/quizzes/micro-expressions-test/
- Paul Ekman Group: Facial Expression Quiz (“What’s that facial expression?”): https://www.paulekman.com/quizzes/whats-that-facial-expression-test-quiz/

### “Reading the Mind in the Eyes” (RMET) interactive
- RMET interactive (launch): https://s3.amazonaws.com/he-assets-prod/interactives/233_reading_the_mind_through_eyes/Launch.html

Teaching use
- Use as a measurement lab: accuracy as the dependent variable.
- Debrief construct boundaries: “emotion recognition” versus “theory of mind” versus vocabulary demands.

Student data capture (choose one)
- Total correct.
- Accuracy by emotion category (if you assign a short coding sheet).

## Lab set C: Affect and emotion regulation as measurement labs (scored scales with feedback)

### Momentary affect (positive vs negative affect; fast; scored feedback)
- PsyToolkit PANAS (Positive and Negative Affect Schedule): https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/panas.html
- PsyToolkit SPANE (positive/negative feelings; affect balance): https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/spane.html

### Emotion regulation strategies (reappraisal vs suppression; scored feedback)
- PsyToolkit ERQ (Emotion Regulation Questionnaire): https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/emotional-regulation-erq.html

### Trait-style “emotion skill” measurement (use carefully; debrief construct validity)
- PsyToolkit Emotional Intelligence scale (Schutte-based): https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/emotional-intelligence.html

Teaching use
- Treat these as measurement labs about operationalization, reliability, and interpretation.
- Avoid making graded claims about personal emotional functioning; grade completion and methodological reflection.

Student data capture (choose one)
- One subscale score (e.g., ERQ reappraisal) plus one limitation statement.
- One difference score (e.g., SPANE-P minus SPANE-N) plus one prediction about behavior.

## Teaching designs that feel like real online labs

### Option A: 50-minute station rotation (high engagement, low setup)
- Station 1: Dot-Probe (attention bias; RT/accuracy)
- Station 2: Social Intelligence Test or RMET (emotion perception; accuracy)
- Station 3: ERQ (regulation strategies; subscale scores)
- Station 4: Micro-expressions or GGSC quiz (recognition under time pressure; score)

Whole-class synthesis prompt
- “What did each lab treat as ‘emotion skill’ (attention, recognition, regulation), and what are the tradeoffs of each operational definition?”

### Option B: Hypothesis-first mini-study (teaches scientific reasoning inside the content)
- Pre-specify a hypothesis: “Higher reappraisal scores predict higher emotion recognition accuracy (or fewer attentional-bias effects).”
- Collect: ERQ subscales + one recognition score + one Dot-Probe metric.
- Debrief: Effect size, confounds (device, distractions), and construct mismatch.

### Low-friction grading pattern (scales to large enrollments)
- One required number per lab (score or difference score).
- One required methodological sentence per lab:
  - IV (or condition difference), DV, and one validity threat.

## Practical cautions (instructional, not clinical)
- Do not require personal disclosure about trauma, depression, anxiety, or therapy experiences.
- If any activity feels personally intrusive for a student, provide a vignette-based alternative (analyze a provided “participant” result screenshot instead).
```

### Block 45 (lang=markdown | source=md | block 45)

```markdown
---
title: "PSY-132 Interactive Teaching Labs (Free Only): Social"
filename: "psy-132_topic-19_social_interactive-teaching-labs_free"
type: "resource_links"
status: "curated"
created: 2026-01-09
updated: 2026-01-09
origin: "Curated for PSY-132 Topic 19 with strict emphasis on free, student-facing interactive lab activities that produce debriefable outcomes (choices, scores, or branching decisions)."
citation_style: "APA"
verification_needed: false
tags:
  - psy-132
  - topic-19
  - social
  - interactive-labs
  - free
topics:
  - "PSY-132"
  - "Social"
aliases:
  - "Topic 19 social labs"
sources:
  - "https://economics-games.com/games"
  - "https://web.mit.edu/bystanders/interactive/index.html"
  - "https://implicit.harvard.edu/implicit/takeatest.html"
  - "https://lioness-lab.org/"
cssclasses: []
notes: "Free-only interactive teaching labs for Social Psychology. Emphasis on multiplayer social dilemmas, branching bystander scenarios, and reaction-time attitude tasks. Use neutral framing and avoid graded personal disclosure."
---

## Selection rationale
- These are free, browser-based activities designed to be *run* (not read) and are easy to debrief as social psych phenomena.
- Each activity yields a clean output you can treat as lab data: choices/payoffs, scenario decisions, or reaction-time–based categorization results.

## Lab set A: Multiplayer social dilemma labs (best for live class, high engagement)

### Economics Games (free, ready to play, nothing to install, no registration)
- Games hub (launch and generate student access): https://economics-games.com/games citeturn1search1
- Repeated Prisoner’s Dilemma (cooperate vs compete; repeated rounds): https://economics-games.com/prisoner-dilemma citeturn0search4
- Public Good Financing Experiment (contribute vs free-ride; repeated rounds): https://economics-games.com/public-good citeturn1search6
- Tragedy of the Commons (shared resource harvesting; repeated rounds): https://economics-games.com/tragedy-commons citeturn1search2
- oTree games pack on the same platform (Ultimatum and other interaction games): https://economics-games.com/otree citeturn1search8

Why this works as a social-psych lab
- You can operationalize social concepts directly: cooperation, reciprocity, norm enforcement, trust, fairness, and collective action problems.

Easy data capture
- One number per student per game (final payoff, average contribution, or cooperation rate across rounds).
- One class-level pattern (mean contribution by round; cooperation rate after betrayal; resource collapse time).

## Lab set B: Bystander effect and social influence (branching scenario lab)

### MIT Active Bystanders interactive scenarios (choose options, see consequences)
- Interactive scenarios hub: https://web.mit.edu/bystanders/interactive/index.html citeturn0search1

Why this works as a social-psych lab
- This is a structured decision-path lab: students choose actions, observe outcomes, and can analyze barriers to intervention (diffusion of responsibility, fear of evaluation, ambiguity).

Easy data capture
- Require one screenshot of the decision path taken plus one coded label:
  - “direct intervention,” “delegation,” “distraction,” “delay,” or “no action.”

## Lab set C: Attitudes, prejudice, and implicit cognition (reaction-time lab)

### Project Implicit IAT (students select a topic; timed categorization; immediate feedback)
- Take an IAT (computer): https://implicit.harvard.edu/implicit/takeatest.html citeturn1search0
- Blindspot-hosted Race IAT direct entry (useful when you want everyone on the same task): https://implicit.harvard.edu/implicit/user/agg/blindspot/indexrk.htm citeturn1search23

Why this works as a social-psych lab
- It is a rare intro-level activity that behaves like a real cognitive task: timed trials, measurable performance, and a direct bridge to attitude measurement and social cognition.

Easy data capture
- Do not grade on “result direction.” Grade on:
  - completion confirmation + one methodological reflection: “What could distort RT-based measures?”

## Lab set D: Build-your-own interactive group experiment (free platform; more instructor setup)

### LIONESS Lab (free web-based platform for interactive online experiments)
- Platform: https://lioness-lab.org/ citeturn0search2
- Documentation: https://lioness-doc.readthedocs.io/ citeturn0search10

Why this works as a teaching lab
- If you want *true* interactive social experiments (group formation, synchronous interaction, dropout handling), LIONESS is purpose-built for that. citeturn0search6

Best use in PSY-132 (pragmatic)
- Use LIONESS when you want a “capstone lab day” with one carefully chosen interactive experiment rather than many small demos.

## Teaching ideas (concrete, low-friction)

### Option A: 50-minute live class “social dilemmas” lab (high yield)
- Run Public Good for 6–8 rounds.
- Then run Prisoner’s Dilemma for 6–8 rounds.
- Debrief:
  - What changed across rounds?
  - Did cooperation stabilize, collapse, or oscillate?
  - What informal norms emerged (punishment, forgiveness, retaliation)?

Minimal grading artifact
- One number (average contribution OR cooperation rate) + one paragraph interpreting the pattern.

### Option B: 50-minute “bystander decision lab” (good for discussion sections)
- Students complete one MIT scenario individually.
- In small groups, compare paths and code each decision into a strategy category.
- Whole-class synthesis:
  - identify the most common failure point (where intervention stops).

Minimal grading artifact
- One screenshot + one coded category + one “what would you do differently and why” sentence.

### Option C: Asynchronous online lab (works in D2L without coordination)
- Assign one IAT topic (same for everyone).
- Require:
  - completion, plus
  - one paragraph on construct validity, demand characteristics, device differences, and interpretation limits.

Minimal grading artifact
- A standardized “methods debrief” template response (same prompts for everyone).

### Lab-to-stats bridge (reuse in Topic 4)
- Public Goods: graph mean contribution by round and discuss trends, variability, and outliers.
- Prisoner’s Dilemma: compute cooperation rate and compare sections (difference in proportions).
- IAT: discuss measurement noise, reaction-time distributions, and why median often beats mean.
```

### Block 46 (lang=markdown | source=md | block 46)

```markdown
---
title: "PSY-132 Interactive Teaching Labs (Free Only): Personality"
filename: "psy-132_topic-20_personality_interactive-teaching-labs_free"
type: "resource_links"
status: "curated"
created: 2026-01-09
updated: 2026-01-09
origin: "Curated for PSY-132 Topic 20 with strict emphasis on free, student-facing interactive lab activities (scored measures and interpretable outputs)."
citation_style: "APA"
verification_needed: false
tags:
  - psy-132
  - topic-20
  - personality
  - interactive-labs
  - free
topics:
  - "PSY-132"
  - "Personality"
aliases:
  - "Topic 20 personality labs"
sources:
  - "https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/"
  - "https://openpsychometrics.org/tests/IPIP-BFFM/"
  - "https://www.personalityassessor.com/bigfive/"
cssclasses: []
notes: "All links below are selected for lab-style teaching: students generate scored outputs (subscales, percentiles, difference scores) that support measurement-focused debriefs. Grade completion and methods reflection, not trait levels."
---

## Selection rationale
- Free and student-facing.
- Interactive in the lab sense: produces scores, subscales, or percentile feedback that can be analyzed.
- Supports Intro Psych goals: traits, measurement, reliability and validity, response bias, and trait versus state.

## Core lab hubs (reusable all semester)

### PsyToolkit survey library (ready-to-run peer-reviewed scales with scoring)
- Survey library hub: https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/

### Open-Source Psychometrics Project (interactive tests with results pages and percentiles)
- Test hub: https://openpsychometrics.org/

### Personality Assessor (free Big Five tests with facets)
- Big Five Inventory 2 (BFI-2) test: https://www.personalityassessor.com/bigfive/

## Lab set A: Big Five traits (the anchor personality lab)

### Very short Big Five (good for same-day class dataset)
- PsyToolkit TIPI (10 items): https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/big5-tipi.html

Use case
- Fast in-class measurement lab and quick introduction to operationalizing traits.

### Short but more stable Big Five (better for debrief)
- PsyToolkit BFI-S (15 items): https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/big5-bfi-s.html

Use case
- Better signal than TIPI while still short enough for a lab day.

### Full Big Five with percentiles (useful for interpreting norms and distributions)
- OpenPsychometrics IPIP Big Five Factor Markers: https://openpsychometrics.org/tests/IPIP-BFFM/
- Results page format (percentiles and raw scores): https://openpsychometrics.org/tests/IPIP-BFFM/results.php

Use case
- Best for a “what does a score mean” discussion and for showing percentiles.

### Big Five with facets (shows how broad traits break down)
- Personality Assessor BFI-2 (domains plus facets): https://www.personalityassessor.com/bigfive/

Use case
- Strong for demonstrating that “Agreeableness” is a bundle of narrower tendencies.

## Lab set B: Self-concept and control beliefs (classic trait-adjacent measures)

### Self-esteem: trait versus state comparison lab
- PsyToolkit Rosenberg Self-Esteem Scale (trait-leaning): https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/self-esteem-rosenberg.html
- PsyToolkit State Self-Esteem Scale (facets; momentary): https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/self-esteem-sses.html

Use case
- Students compare score stability logic and interpret why state measures can shift with context.

### Locus of control and perceived control (clean for prediction and debrief)
- PsyToolkit Rotter Locus of Control: https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/locus-of-control-rotter.html
- PsyToolkit Spheres of Control Scale (domain-specific control): https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/spheres-of-control-scale.html

Use case
- Demonstrates that “control” can differ by life domain, which reduces simplistic trait interpretations.

## Lab set C: Persistence and self-regulation as measurable traits

### Grit (works as a measurement and prediction lab)
- PsyToolkit Short Grit Scale (Grit-S): https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/grit-short.html

Use case
- Pairs well with later discussions of motivation, achievement, and confounds.

## Teaching ideas that keep this lab-like

## Option A: Measurement lab day (50 minutes, minimal setup)
- Step 1: Run TIPI or BFI-S for everyone.
- Step 2: Run Rosenberg and State Self-Esteem back-to-back.
- Step 3: Run Rotter Locus of Control.
- Step 4: Students submit three numbers and one methods note:
  - Big Five: one trait score (or two if you prefer)
  - Self-esteem: trait score and state score
  - Locus of control: internal-external score
  - One validity threat (device, distractions, response bias, misunderstanding items)

Debrief prompts
- What is the construct and what is the operational definition here.
- What would change if the instructions asked for “answer honestly” versus “answer like an ideal job applicant.”
- Which measure looks most sensitive to immediate context and why.

## Option B: Response-bias lab (very teachable, low risk)
- Randomly assign half the class:
  - Condition 1: answer honestly
  - Condition 2: answer as if applying for a competitive scholarship or job
- Use BFI-S or Rosenberg.
- Compare class distributions by condition.

Debrief prompts
- What kind of bias does the instruction manipulation induce.
- What does that imply about self-report measurement in personality research.

## Option C: Reliability mini-lab (makes “traits are stable” concrete)
- Run BFI-S in Week A and again in Week B.
- Students compute a simple stability index for one trait:
  - absolute difference between Week A and Week B
- Aggregate differences and discuss measurement error and stability.

## Easy grading pattern that scales
- Grade completion and the methods reflection.
- Avoid grading on trait levels or requiring personal explanations.
- Allow an opt-out alternative: analyze a provided “participant results” screenshot instead of submitting personal scores.
---
```

### Block 47 (lang=markdown | source=md | block 47)

```markdown
---
title: "PSY-132 Interactive Teaching Labs: Gender"
filename: "psy-132_topic-21_gender_interactive-teaching-labs_free"
type: "resource_links"
status: "curated"
created: 2026-01-09
updated: 2026-01-09
origin: "Curated for PSY-132 Topic 21 with strict emphasis on free, student-facing interactive lab activities that produce debriefable outputs (reaction time, scores, classifications)."
citation_style: "APA"
verification_needed: false
tags:
  - psy-132
  - topic-21
  - gender
  - interactive-labs
  - free
topics:
  - "PSY-132"
  - "Gender"
aliases:
  - "Topic 21 gender labs"
sources:
  - "https://implicit.harvard.edu/implicit/takeatest.html"
  - "https://implicit.harvard.edu/implicit/user/agg/blindspot/indexgc.htm"
  - "https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/sex-role-bem.html"
  - "https://openpsychometrics.org/tests/OSRI/"
  - "https://openpsychometrics.org/tests/FPS.php"
  - "https://gender-decoder.katmatfield.com/"
cssclasses: []
notes: "Free-only interactive teaching labs for Gender. Grade completion and methodological reflection, not trait levels or belief content. Provide an opt-out alternative such as analyzing a provided screenshot or vignette."
---

## Selection rationale
- Every activity below is interactive and produces an output you can treat as lab data: reaction time feedback, a score profile, or a coded text result.
- The set covers three complementary lab modes that work well in Intro Psych.
- Cognitive task lab (implicit association).
- Measurement lab (gender-role and gender-attitude scales).
- Applied language lab (gender-coded wording in real texts).

## Lab set A: Implicit gender stereotypes as a timed cognitive task (reaction-time lab)

### Gender–Career / Family Implicit Association Test (direct entry)
- Gender–Career IAT (Blindspot entry): https://implicit.harvard.edu/implicit/user/agg/blindspot/indexgc.htm

### General IAT selector (if you want students to choose from the menu)
- Take an IAT (computer): https://implicit.harvard.edu/implicit/takeatest.html
- Take an IAT (touch/tablet): https://implicit.harvard.edu/implicit/takeatouchtestv2.html

Teaching use
- Treat this as a measurement lab about reaction-time categorization under different pairing rules.
- Debrief what the task is optimized to measure, and what it is not optimized to measure.

Student data capture options
- Completion confirmation plus screenshot of the final feedback screen.
- One methodological reflection sentence: device type, distractions, prior familiarity, speed–accuracy tradeoff.

## Lab set B: Gender roles and gender expression as measurable constructs (scored survey labs)

### Bem Sex Role Inventory (BSRI) on PsyToolkit (classic role-endorsement measure)
- BSRI (PsyToolkit): https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/sex-role-bem.html

### Open Sex-Role Inventory (OSRI) on OpenPsychometrics (two-scale masculinity and femininity output)
- OSRI start page: https://openpsychometrics.org/tests/OSRI/
- OSRI interactive item stream (direct): https://openpsychometrics.org/tests/OSRI/1.php

Teaching use
- Compare how BSRI and OSRI operationalize gendered traits.
- Focus on construct definition and the difference between gender identity and endorsement of gender-role stereotypes.

Student data capture options
- Two subscale scores only, no narrative explanation.
- One sentence labeling a validity limitation of gender-role inventories.

## Lab set C: Gender attitudes as measurable beliefs (scored attitude lab)

### Feminist Perspectives Scale (FPS) on OpenPsychometrics
- FPS (interactive): https://openpsychometrics.org/tests/FPS.php

Teaching use
- Treat as an attitudes measurement lab.
- Focus on how item wording, social desirability, and context shape responding.

Student data capture options
- Total score plus one statement about response bias risk.

## Lab set D: Gender-coded language in texts (applied analysis lab)

### Gender Decoder (paste text, get coded-word counts and an overall classification)
- Gender Decoder tool: https://gender-decoder.katmatfield.com/
- About (method and word lists): https://gender-decoder.katmatfield.com/about
- FAQ (interpretation guardrails): https://gender-decoder.katmatfield.com/faq

Teaching use
- Run as a “language operationalization” lab.
- Students test multiple texts and compare how coding shifts with edits.

Student data capture options
- One before-and-after screenshot showing the coded-word counts.
- One sentence identifying the independent variable as the edit and the dependent variable as the coded-word balance.

## Optional instructor-preview lab (content can be inflammatory, use with care)

### Hypermasculinity Values Questionnaire (HVQ) on PsyToolkit
- HVQ (PsyToolkit): https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/hypermasculinity-hvq.html

Teaching use
- Use only if you want to explicitly discuss hostile gender norms and socialization.
- Provide an opt-out alternative by default.

## Teaching designs and ideas

## Option A: 50-minute station rotation (high engagement, minimal setup)
- Station 1: Gender–Career IAT
- Station 2: BSRI or OSRI (pick one for time)
- Station 3: Gender Decoder (text analysis)
- Station 4: FPS attitude scale (optional if time allows)

Whole-class synthesis
- What is each lab measuring.
- What is each lab not measuring.
- How would two students reach similar outputs for different reasons.

## Option B: Methods-first mini-study (teaches research logic inside the topic)
- Pre-specify a hypothesis in class:
  - Higher endorsement of traditional gender roles predicts stronger gender–career pairing effects.
- Collect only minimal variables:
  - One gender-role score and one IAT completion screenshot.
- Debrief threats to validity:
  - device differences, distractions, social desirability, and construct mismatch.

## Low-friction grading pattern that scales
- Grade completion and a short methods reflection.
- Require one numeric output per activity and one sentence labeling IV and DV.
- Provide an alternative assignment: interpret a provided results screenshot instead of generating personal results.
```

### Block 48 (lang=markdown | source=md | block 48)

```markdown
---
title: "PSY-132 Interactive Teaching Labs (Free Only): Multicultural"
filename: "psy-132_topic-22_multicultural_interactive-teaching-labs_free"
type: "resource_links"
status: "curated"
created: 2026-01-09
updated: 2026-01-09
origin: "Curated for PSY-132 Topic 22 with strict emphasis on free, student-facing interactive lab activities (timed tasks, scored measures, and interactive cross-cultural datasets/tools)."
citation_style: "APA"
verification_needed: false
tags:
  - psy-132
  - topic-22
  - multicultural
  - cross-cultural
  - interactive-labs
  - free
topics:
  - "PSY-132"
  - "Multicultural"
aliases:
  - "Topic 22 multicultural labs"
sources:
  - "https://implicit.harvard.edu/implicit/takeatest.html"
  - "https://studies.labinthewild.org/highlow/"
  - "https://www.worldvaluessurvey.org/WVSOnline.jsp"
  - "https://www.pewresearch.org/international-question-search/"
  - "https://geerthofstede.com/country-comparison-bar-charts/"
  - "https://map.yourmorals.org/"
  - "https://www.yourmorals.org/"
cssclasses: []
notes: "All activities below are free and lab-like: students generate interpretable outputs (reaction time feedback, scores, or interactive cross-cultural comparisons). Grade completion + methods reasoning, not personal beliefs or ‘bias direction.’ Provide an opt-out path (analyze a provided screenshot/dataset instead of generating personal results)."
---

## Selection rationale
- Free, browser-based activities that students can actually run.
- Outputs you can treat as data: reaction time feedback, scale scores, country comparisons, or interactive cross-tabs.
- Supports Intro Psych goals: operationalizing culture, avoiding essentialism, and separating within-group variability from between-group averages.

## Lab set A: Implicit cultural associations (timed cognitive task; measurable output)
- Project Implicit: Take an IAT (computer): https://implicit.harvard.edu/implicit/takeatest.html
- Project Implicit: Take an IAT (alternate selector page): https://implicit.harvard.edu/implicit/takeatestv2.html
- About the IAT (for debrief framing): https://implicit.harvard.edu/implicit/iatdetails.html

Teaching use
- Treat as a measurement lab: timed categorization under different pairing rules.
- Use culturally relevant topics (race, skin tone, religion, nationality) based on what the menu currently offers.

Student data capture (low risk)
- Screenshot of the completion/feedback screen plus one methods paragraph:
  - device type, distractions, speed–accuracy tradeoff, and one limitation of RT-based measures.

## Lab set B: Cross-cultural communication style (scored output; interpretation lab)
- Lab in the Wild: High-Context vs Low-Context Communication test: https://studies.labinthewild.org/highlow/
- Lab in the Wild hub (optional additional studies): https://labinthewild.org/

Teaching use
- Use as a “construct operationalization” lab: communication style as a measurable dimension.
- Debrief why the construct is probabilistic (not a label for individuals or whole cultures).

Student data capture
- One score/output statement plus one “context caveat” sentence:
  - “What part of your life context might push you more high- or low-context?”

## Lab set C: Cross-national values and norms (interactive dataset analysis; real survey data)
- World Values Survey Online Data Analysis tool: https://www.worldvaluessurvey.org/WVSOnline.jsp

Teaching use
- This is your strongest “data lab” for multicultural content:
  - students choose a wave, pick a variable, compare countries, and interpret patterns.
- Debrief sampling, measurement equivalence, and the “mean vs distribution” problem.

Student data capture
- Require one exportable artifact:
  - screenshot of the comparison table/plot plus the exact question/variable label and selected countries.

## Lab set D: Cross-national attitudes question search (interactive item bank; build a mini-study)
- Pew Research Center: International Question Search: https://www.pewresearch.org/international-question-search/

Teaching use
- Run as a “survey design lab”:
  - students locate one recurring cross-national item (e.g., trust, immigration, religion, gender roles), then propose a hypothesis and identify which countries/years to compare.
- Debrief wording effects and comparability across translations/cultures.

Student data capture
- One item prompt plus one proposed operational definition:
  - what is the DV, what are the comparison groups, and what confound is most likely.

## Lab set E: Cultural-dimensions comparison (interactive visualization; debrief carefully)
- Hofstede-based country comparison bar charts (interactive tool): https://geerthofstede.com/country-comparison-bar-charts/
- Optional alternate view: country comparison graphs (2-D + color shading): https://geerthofstede.com/country-comparison-graphs/
- Optional interactive globe: Hofstede’s Globe: https://geerthofstede.com/hofstedes-globe/

Teaching use
- Use only as a modeling/visualization lab, not as “explaining individuals.”
- Debrief ecological fallacy and within-country variance (country averages are not people).

Student data capture
- Pick 3 countries and capture:
  - one screenshot + one claim written as a conditional and a limitation statement.

## Lab set F: Moral values geography and variation (interactive map + optional test)
- MapYourMorals (interactive moral geography map): https://map.yourmorals.org/
- YourMorals (optional: take a scale; requires registration): https://www.yourmorals.org/

Teaching use
- Use to teach within-culture variability:
  - regional clusters, prediction uncertainty, and the danger of “culture as monolith.”
- Pair with WVS to contrast within-country vs between-country comparisons.

Student data capture
- One map screenshot + one interpretation sentence that avoids overgeneralization:
  - “This shows a modeled estimate for region-level patterns; it does not determine any individual’s values.”

## Teaching designs and ideas

## Option A: 50-minute station rotation (high engagement, minimal setup)
- Station 1: IAT (implicit associations and measurement limits).
- Station 2: High/Low Context test (construct measurement).
- Station 3: WVS Online Analysis (country comparison with real survey data).
- Station 4: Pew International Question Search (build a hypothesis and identify a testable comparison).

Deliverable (simple, scalable)
- One screenshot per station plus one “IV/DV + limitation” sentence per station.

## Option B: One-week asynchronous online lab (D2L-friendly)
- Students complete WVS Online Analysis using a provided checklist:
  - choose wave, choose variable, pick 4 countries, record pattern, state one confound.
- Students then use Pew Question Search to find a related item and predict whether it would match the WVS pattern.

Deliverable
- Two screenshots and a short methods paragraph comparing the two sources.

## Option C: Mini-study that teaches inference without claiming causal culture effects
- Pre-specify:
  - “Country-level values will correlate with country-level attitudes, but the relationship will vary by region and item wording.”
- Use WVS for values and Pew for attitudes; compare whether patterns align.
- Debrief:
  - correlation vs causation, ecological fallacy, measurement equivalence.

## Debrief prompts that force lab thinking
- What is the independent variable (country, wave, condition, item wording, task pairing)?
- What is the dependent variable (score, RT feedback, response distribution, comparison statistic)?
- What is one alternative explanation (translation, sampling, selection bias, device differences, demand characteristics)?
- What claim would be invalid because it jumps from group averages to individual people?
```

### Block 49 (lang=markdown | source=md | block 49)

```markdown
---
title: "PSY-132 Interactive Teaching Labs (Free Only): Abnormal"
filename: "psy-132_topic-23_abnormal_interactive-teaching-labs_free"
type: "resource_links"
status: "curated"
created: 2026-01-09
updated: 2026-01-09
origin: "Curated for PSY-132 Topic 23 with strict emphasis on free, student-facing interactive lab activities that generate debriefable outputs."
citation_style: "APA"
verification_needed: false
tags:
  - psy-132
  - topic-23
  - abnormal
  - interactive-labs
  - free
topics:
  - "PSY-132"
  - "Abnormal"
aliases:
  - "Topic 23 abnormal labs"
sources:
  - "https://screening.mhanational.org/screening-tools/"
  - "https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/"
  - "https://implicit.harvard.edu/implicit/user/pih/pih/mentalhealthiats.html"
  - "https://thestarr.org/ahs/"
cssclasses: []
notes: "These are teaching labs, not diagnosis. Default grading should be completion + methods reasoning. Provide an opt-out alternative that uses a provided vignette or screenshot instead of personal responses."
---

## Selection rationale
- Free, browser-based interactivity that behaves like a lab: students produce measurable outputs (scores, subscales, reaction-time feedback, or structured decision results).
- Supports Intro Psych learning goals: operational definitions, symptom measurement, screening versus diagnosis, base rates, false positives/negatives, stigma, and ethics.

## Instructor framing that keeps this academically safe
- Treat all outputs as demonstrations of measurement and screening logic, not as clinical conclusions.
- Avoid requiring personal disclosure. Offer a “vignette mode” option:
  - students score a provided mock response set, or
  - students analyze a provided screenshot of results.
- Grade method and reasoning, not score direction or severity.

## Lab set A: Symptom screening as a measurement lab (interactive, scored outputs)

### Mental Health America screening tools (pick the tests you want the class to run)
- Screening tools hub: https://screening.mhanational.org/screening-tools/
- Depression test: https://screening.mhanational.org/screening-tools/depression/
- Anxiety test: https://screening.mhanational.org/screening-tools/anxiety/
- Bipolar test: https://screening.mhanational.org/screening-tools/bipolar/
- PTSD test: https://screening.mhanational.org/screening-tools/ptsd/
- Psychosis & Schizophrenia test (PQ-B): https://screening.mhanational.org/screening-tools/psychosis/

How this functions like a lab
- Students experience how “symptoms” become scored items and cut-scores.
- You can teach screening logic: sensitivity/specificity, base rate problems, and why screens require follow-up.

Minimal data capture
- One score (or “risk category”) + one limitation statement:
  - “This is a screening tool; only trained clinicians diagnose.”

## Lab set B: Classic clinical scales with explicit scoring (teaching-friendly, research-adjacent)

### PsyToolkit survey library (free; scored scales; good for structured debrief)
- Survey library hub: https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/
- PHQ-9 (depression severity): https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/depression-phq.html
- GAD-7 (anxiety severity): https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/anxiety-gad7.html
- DASS-21 (depression/anxiety/stress): https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/dass21.html
- OCI-R (OCD symptom domains): https://www.psytoolkit.org/survey-library/obsessiveness-oci-r.html

How this functions like a lab
- Students compare instruments (single-construct vs multi-construct) and see how scoring rules affect interpretation.
- You can teach reliability/validity at an intro level by comparing how different measures “slice” distress.

Minimal data capture
- One subscale score (or total score) + one “threat to validity”:
  - device distractions, interpretation of items, social desirability, or transient state effects.

## Lab set C: Stigma and implicit attitudes about mental illness (reaction-time lab)

### Project Implicit Health (mental health IATs)
- Mental Health IATs hub: https://implicit.harvard.edu/implicit/user/pih/pih/mentalhealthiats.html
- Project Implicit Health (general entry): https://implicit.harvard.edu/implicit/user/pih/pih/index.jsp
- Project Implicit Health (alternate “take a test” route): https://www.projectimplicithealth.com/selectiat_desktop.html

How this functions like a lab
- Students see a timed categorization task (not a self-report scale) and learn why RT measures have different limitations than questionnaires.

Minimal data capture
- Completion confirmation + one methods paragraph:
  - device type, distractions, speed–accuracy tradeoff, and one interpretation limit.

## Lab set D: Symptom-experience simulation (strong lab impact; instructor preview recommended)

### Auditory hallucination simulation (STARR Coalition)
- Simulation landing page: https://thestarr.org/ahs/
- Module page: https://thestarr.org/module/auditory-hallucination-simulation/

How this functions like a lab
- Students experience a controlled manipulation (simulated perceptual input) and observe downstream performance effects (attention, frustration, task completion).
- Best for connecting “symptom” to functional impairment and environmental supports.

Minimal data capture
- One structured observation + one accommodation:
  - “What task became harder?” and “What environmental change would reduce impairment?”

## Optional lab add-on (screening, not diagnosis)
- VA National Center for PTSD: PTSD self-screen: https://www.ptsd.va.gov/screen/

## Teaching designs and ideas

## Option A: 50-minute station rotation (high engagement, low setup)
- Station 1: PHQ-9 or DASS-21 (measurement and scoring)
- Station 2: OCI-R (symptom domains and heterogeneity)
- Station 3: Psychosis PQ-B (screening logic and false positives)
- Station 4: Mental Illness IAT (stigma measurement)
- Station 5: Auditory hallucination simulation (function and accommodation)

Deliverable
- One numeric output per station + one sentence labeling:
  - what was measured, and one limitation.

## Option B: Screening-versus-diagnosis mini-study (methods-first)
- Provide 2–3 fictional vignettes.
- Students choose one tool (MHA or PsyToolkit scale) and justify:
  - why it fits the vignette,
  - what it cannot conclude,
  - what follow-up information would be needed for diagnosis.

Deliverable
- Tool choice + scored result + a short “next data needed” list.

## Option C: Build a class dataset for Topic 4 (Statistics) later
- Use only de-identified metrics:
  - PHQ-9 total, GAD-7 total, OCI-R total, PQ-B endorsed count.
- In statistics week:
  - compare distributions, discuss skew, and show why cut-scores can misclassify when base rates are low.

## Debrief prompts that force lab thinking
- What is the operational definition of “severity” in this tool?
- What is the likely source of measurement error?
- What base-rate assumption is hiding under your interpretation?
- What ethical constraint applies if this were a real class study?
---
```

### Block 50 (lang=markdown | source=md | block 50)

```markdown
---
title: "PSY-132 Interactive Teaching Labs (Free Only): Therapies"
filename: "psy-132_topic-24_therapies_interactive-teaching-labs_free"
type: "resource_links"
status: "curated"
created: 2026-01-09
updated: 2026-01-09
origin: "Curated for PSY-132 Topic 24 with strict emphasis on free, student-facing interactive lab activities (branching simulations + skill-building tools that produce debriefable outputs)."
citation_style: "APA"
verification_needed: false
tags:
  - psy-132
  - topic-24
  - therapies
  - interactive-labs
  - free
topics:
  - "PSY-132"
  - "Therapies"
aliases:
  - "Topic 24 therapies labs"
sources:
  - "https://www.wistechopen.org/open-rn-virtual-simulations"
  - "https://studio.libretexts.org/collection/9229"
  - "https://h5p.org/branching-scenario"
  - "https://bohangar.com/cbt-thought-record/"
cssclasses: []
notes: "These are teaching labs, not clinical care. Grade completion and methodological reasoning. Default to vignette-based responses and offer an opt-out alternative for any self-applied tool."
---

## Selection rationale
- Free, browser-based interactivity that behaves like a lab: students make choices, follow branches, complete structured exercises, and produce outputs you can debrief.
- Emphasis on therapy process skills (therapeutic communication, case formulation moves, CBT skill practice explained as operational steps).
- Output is easy to grade without personal disclosure: choice paths, short artifacts (exports), and one-method paragraph.

## Lab set A: Branching “therapy process” simulations (best fit for teaching interactively)

### OpenRN Virtual Simulations (H5P; Creative Commons; reusable in an LMS)
- Scenario list hub: https://www.wistechopen.org/open-rn-virtual-simulations
- OpenRN simulations collection (LibreStudio collection landing page): https://studio.libretexts.org/collection/9229

Recommended OpenRN therapeutic communication scenarios (CC BY)
- Therapeutic Communication – Schizophrenia: https://studio.libretexts.org/h5p/8691
- Therapeutic Communication – Substance Use: https://studio.libretexts.org/h5p/8704
- Therapeutic Communication – Cultural Competency: https://studio.libretexts.org/h5p/8694
- Therapeutic Communication – Sexual Orientation: https://studio.libretexts.org/h5p/8707
- Therapeutic Communication – Pediatric Separation Anxiety: https://studio.libretexts.org/h5p/8692

How this functions like a therapy lab
- Students practice decision points that mirror clinical process (rapport, reflection, validation, boundary setting, harm reduction language).
- Debrief focuses on process: what the “helper” did, what it signaled, and what it changed in the interaction.

Student data capture (low risk)
- One screenshot of the final outcome screen or the choice path.
- One coded label for the primary strategy used:
  - reflection, open question, affirmation, summary, boundary, referral, safety check.

## Lab set B: Build-your-own branching scenario lab (useful when you want exact alignment to Intro Psych)

### H5P Branching Scenario (free HTML5 content type)
- Content type overview: https://h5p.org/branching-scenario
- Example branching scenario (Skills Practice: A Home Visit): https://h5p.org/h5p/embed/439819

How this functions like a teaching lab
- You can create a “micro-therapy” decision lab using fictional vignettes (CBT, MI, supportive counseling), then branch based on student choices.
- This supports evaluation of clinical reasoning without requiring student self-disclosure.

Instructor build idea (minimal)
- One vignette, 5–7 decision points, 2–3 endings.
- Label each decision with the intended skill (OARS, CBT guided discovery move, boundary/safety check).
- Add a final reflection prompt: “Name the skill and the hypothesized mechanism.”

## Lab set C: CBT skills as structured “artifact labs” (students produce a concrete work product)

### Thought record (cognitive restructuring)
- Free CBT Thought Record Worksheet (interactive; export): https://bohangar.com/cbt-thought-record/

Suggested teaching use
- Provide a fictional trigger scenario and a pre-written “automatic thought.”
- Students complete the record using the vignette, then export and submit.

Grading-friendly output
- Exported text file or screenshot.
- One sentence identifying the cognitive distortion or core belief being challenged.

### Exposure planning (graded steps; behavioral treatment planning)
- Exposure Ladder Builder (interactive; export): https://bohangar.com/exposure-ladder-tool/

Suggested teaching use
- Assign a non-sensitive target (public speaking, test anxiety, elevator avoidance, phone calls).
- Students create a 6–10 step ladder with SUDS ratings and a safety plan.

Grading-friendly output
- Ladder screenshot or export.
- One sentence naming the mechanism: extinction/inhibitory learning and reduced avoidance.

### Cognitive distortions practice (classification + reframe prompt)
- Cognitive Distortions Quiz & Coach (interactive; export): https://bohangar.com/cognitive-distortions-quiz-coach-free-cbt-tool/

Suggested teaching use
- Use as a “spot the distortion” station, then require one reframe.
- Keep it vignette-based by providing a list of sample thoughts to evaluate.

Grading-friendly output
- One distortion label + one balanced alternative thought.

## Lab set D: Optional scored “skills check” (use as measurement/debrief, not as authority)

### Cognitive distortions test (scored output)
- IDRlabs Cognitive Distortions Test: https://www.idrlabs.com/cognitive-distortions/test.php

Suggested teaching use
- Use only as a prompt for measurement critique:
  - what is being measured, what is the reference group, what is the validity claim.

Grading-friendly output
- One score category plus one limitation statement (construct validity, response bias, and interpretability).

## Teaching designs and ideas

## Option A: 50-minute station rotation (high engagement, low setup)
- Station 1: OpenRN Therapeutic Communication scenario (pick one).
- Station 2: Thought record (vignette-based).
- Station 3: Exposure ladder (non-sensitive target).
- Station 4: Cognitive distortions coach (apply to provided sample thoughts).
- Whole-class synthesis: “What changed behavior or thinking in each lab, and what mechanism explains that change?”

Deliverable
- One artifact per station (screenshot/export) plus one “skill + mechanism” sentence.

## Option B: Asynchronous online lab (works in D2L without coordination)
- Students complete one OpenRN scenario.
- Students complete one CBT artifact lab (thought record or exposure ladder) using a provided vignette.
- Students submit:
  - the artifact,
  - a short paragraph naming the therapy orientation and mechanism,
  - one alternative explanation or limitation.

## Option C: Treatment matching mini-lab (builds evidence-based thinking without clinical claims)
- Provide three fictional cases (panic, depression, substance use).
- Students choose:
  - one OpenRN scenario for process skills, plus
  - one CBT artifact tool that fits the case.
- Debrief focuses on match quality and mechanism, not on “diagnosing.”

## Debrief prompts that keep it lab-like
- What was the independent variable (choice made, step taken, reframe used)?
- What was the dependent variable (scenario outcome, SUDS plan, re-rated emotion, quality of client response)?
- What mechanism is claimed (reinforcement, cognitive change, exposure learning, alliance/process factors)?
- What limitation matters (measurement, generalizability, demand characteristics, context)?
```

### Block 51 (lang=markdown | source=md | block 51)

```markdown
---
title: "PSY-132 Interactive Teaching Labs Acceptance Test"
filename: "psy-132_interactive-teaching-labs_acceptance-test_free-only"
type: "standard"
status: "draft"
created: 2026-01-09
updated: 2026-01-09
origin: "Derived from iterative constraints used while curating PSY-132 topic link sets"
citation_style: "APA"
verification_needed: false
tags:
  - psy-132
  - standards
  - interactive-labs
  - acceptance-test
  - free
topics:
  - "PSY-132"
aliases:
  - "Interactive lab link acceptance test"
sources: []
cssclasses: []
notes: "Mechanical checklist for deciding whether a link qualifies as a free, student-facing, teaching-interactive lab for PSY-132."
---

## Purpose
Use this checklist to decide whether a web resource qualifies for inclusion in PSY-132 as an interactive teaching lab link under a strict “free only” constraint.

## Definition
A qualifying link is a student-facing activity that runs with low friction and produces an output that supports a lab-style debrief, including independent variables, dependent variables, and validity limitations.

## Acceptance test checklist

## A. Access and cost
- The activity is free for students to run without a paid license, paid code, or institution subscription.
- The activity does not require a paywall after a short demo that would block normal completion.
- The activity does not require installation of proprietary software as a condition of completion.
  - Mobile apps may be included only if a web alternative does not exist and the app is free.

## B. Friction and compatibility
- The activity runs in a modern browser on common student devices.
  - It should work on at least one of: Chromebook, Windows laptop, Mac laptop, iOS, Android.
- The activity does not require deprecated plugins or runtimes.
  - Exclude Java applets, Flash, and similar legacy dependencies.
- The activity works without requiring account creation.
  - If an account is required, include only if the activity is unusually high value and the account is free and low-friction.

## C. Interactivity threshold
- The student must actively do something beyond reading or watching.
  - Examples: respond to stimuli, manipulate parameters, make repeated choices, drag-and-drop, complete a branching scenario.
- The activity includes at least one of:
  - timed responses
  - repeated trials
  - parameter manipulation with visible outcome changes
  - decision branches with consequences
  - structured item responses with scoring

## D. Lab output requirement
At least one of the following must be produced, and it must be recordable:
- reaction time, accuracy, error rate, or trial feedback
- a score or subscale profile with scoring rules or interpretability cues
- a graph or model output that changes when parameters change
- a decision path plus an outcome screen
- a coded text result with counts or classifications

## E. Debriefability
- The activity supports a clean identification of:
  - what can function as an independent variable, even if implicit in conditions or trial types
  - what is measured as the dependent variable
- The activity supports at least one standard intro-psych validity discussion:
  - measurement error
  - construct validity
  - demand characteristics
  - sampling/selection bias
  - confounds such as device differences, distractions, or prior familiarity

## F. Classroom scalability
- Students can complete the activity in a predictable time window.
  - Default target: 5–20 minutes for an in-class station.
  - Longer activities are acceptable only if they are clearly worth a full lab day.
- The activity supports simple submission evidence with minimal disputes.
  - Preferred: one screenshot of results plus one numeric or categorical output.
- The activity does not require synchronous coordination unless it is explicitly a multiplayer class experiment.
  - If synchronous, it must have an instructor-run flow that works in a typical class session.

## G. Safety and student privacy
- The activity does not require students to disclose personal mental health information, trauma, substance use, or sensitive life history for grading.
- If the activity is a self-report scale on sensitive content, inclusion requires:
  - default vignette-based option, or
  - opt-out alternative using a provided screenshot or mock dataset
  - grading based on completion and methods reasoning, not score level
- The activity includes no manipulative persuasion, harassment, or content that would reasonably create harm without clear instructor framing.
  - If content could be disturbing, label it instructor-preview and require an opt-out path.

## Decision rule
- Include the link if it passes all of A, C, D, and G, and at least three of B, E, and F.
- Exclude the link if it fails any of A, C, D, or G.

## Required metadata for each included link
- Link title
- URL
- Activity type
  - cognitive task, simulation, branching scenario, scored scale, multiplayer experiment, interactive dataset tool, text-coding tool
- Output type
  - RT/accuracy, score/subscales, graph/model, decision path, coded counts
- Typical completion time estimate
- Submission evidence format
  - screenshot, numeric output, brief reflection
- Notes for instructor framing
  - required caveats and any opt-out alternative
---
```

### Block 53 (lang=markdown | source=md | block 53)

```markdown
---
title: "PSY-132 Interactive Teaching Labs: History"
filename: "psy-132-topic-1-history-interactive-teaching-labs-free"
type: "resource_links"
status: "curated"
created: "2026-01-11"
updated: "2026-01-11"
origin: "Curated for PSY-132 Topic 1 with strict emphasis on free, student-facing interactive teaching labs"
citation_style: "none"
verification_needed: false
tags:
  - "psy-132"
  - "history"
  - "interactive-labs"
  - "free"
topics:
  - "History"
aliases: []
sources:
  - "https://www.learner.org/series/discovering-psychology/explorations/history-of-psychology-contemporary-foundations/"
  - "https://www.apa.org/about/apa/archives/timeline"
  - "https://www.uakron.edu/chp/education/online-exhibits"
  - "https://www.uakron.edu/chp/museum/"
  - "https://www.uakron.edu/chp/images/ScavengerHunt_short_final2018.pdf"
  - "https://www.uakron.edu/chp/museum/A%20Guide%20for%20Educators_National%20Museum%20of%20Psychology%20-%202022%20Edit.pdf?language_id=1"
  - "https://learninglab.si.edu/collections/exploring-heredity-race-eugenics-and-the-history-of-intelligence-testing/ytlaOzLVuq36ejHP"
  - "https://psychology.barnard.edu/psych-psych-virtual-museum"
  - "https://www.uakron.edu/chp/education/videos.dot"
  - "https://www.uakron.edu/chp/education/pdfs/Episode7_TeachersGuide_5MHL.pdf"
cssclasses: []
notes: "History rarely produces reaction-time data, so the lab outputs here are coded responses, timeline choices, scavenger-hunt answers, and artifact analyses. Grade completion plus methods reasoning, not opinions."
---

## Selection rationale
- Free, browser-based resources that students actively navigate, classify, and extract evidence from
- Activities produce recordable outputs suitable for grading at scale
- Designed to support debriefs about how the field changed over time and why

## Interactive teaching labs

## Lab 1: Interactive timeline scavenger hunt (Annenberg Learner)
- Link
  - https://www.learner.org/series/discovering-psychology/explorations/history-of-psychology-contemporary-foundations/
- Activity type
  - Interactive timeline exploration
- Output type
  - Coded event selections and short written evidence claims
- Typical completion time
  - 10–20 minutes
- Submission evidence format
  - Screenshot of two selected timeline entries
  - Three-item response set: date, person or concept, why it matters
- Instructor framing notes
  - Require students to label each entry as one of: method shift, theory shift, institutional milestone, applied use case

## Lab 2: Interactive institutional timeline (APA History and Archives)
- Link
  - https://www.apa.org/about/apa/archives/timeline
- Activity type
  - Interactive timeline with milestone filtering
- Output type
  - Event selection plus classification code
- Typical completion time
  - 10–15 minutes
- Submission evidence format
  - Screenshot of one milestone
  - One-paragraph “context note” using only what is on the page
- Instructor framing notes
  - Use to teach the difference between “psychology as a science” and “psychology as a profession”

## Lab 3: Online exhibits with built-in classroom activities (Cummings Center, University of Akron)
- Link
  - https://www.uakron.edu/chp/education/online-exhibits
- Activity type
  - Curated exhibit plus attached teaching resources
- Output type
  - Scavenger hunt answers and primary-source evidence extraction
- Typical completion time
  - 20–30 minutes per exhibit
- Submission evidence format
  - One completed scavenger hunt page or answer set
  - One “source card” with: artifact title, what it is, what claim it supports
- Instructor framing notes
  - Require students to separate description from interpretation
  - Description: what the artifact literally shows
  - Interpretation: what it suggests about psychology at the time

## Lab 4: Museum scavenger hunt as a structured history lab (National Museum of Psychology)
- Core museum page
  - https://www.uakron.edu/chp/museum/
- Scavenger hunt PDF
  - https://www.uakron.edu/chp/images/ScavengerHunt_short_final2018.pdf
- Educator guide PDF
  - https://www.uakron.edu/chp/museum/A%20Guide%20for%20Educators_National%20Museum%20of%20Psychology%20-%202022%20Edit.pdf?language_id=1
- Activity type
  - Guided artifact search and matching task
- Output type
  - Completed matching and short-response prompts
- Typical completion time
  - 20–40 minutes
- Submission evidence format
  - Photo or scan of completed scavenger hunt page
  - One “most surprising exhibit” note with a single supported claim
- Instructor framing notes
  - This works in-person or virtual if you provide screenshots of a few exhibits

## Lab 5: Curated collection lab (Smithsonian Learning Lab)
- Link
  - https://learninglab.si.edu/collections/exploring-heredity-race-eugenics-and-the-history-of-intelligence-testing/ytlaOzLVuq36ejHP
- Activity type
  - Curated collection navigation and annotation
- Output type
  - Item selection plus evidence-based critique
- Typical completion time
  - 20–30 minutes
- Submission evidence format
  - Select two items and submit a “two-column analysis”
  - Column 1: what the item is
  - Column 2: what claim it supports or complicates
- Instructor framing notes
  - Require a bias and ethics note: “What harm could follow from misusing this idea or tool?”

## Lab 6: Artifact analysis lab (Barnard Psychology Virtual Museum)
- Link
  - https://psychology.barnard.edu/psych-psych-virtual-museum
- Activity type
  - Artifact browsing and structured analysis
- Output type
  - “instrument inference” worksheet
- Typical completion time
  - 15–25 minutes
- Submission evidence format
  - Choose one apparatus and answer four prompts
  - What is it
  - What might it measure
  - What assumption about mind or behavior is embedded in it
  - What would count as good evidence in that paradigm
- Instructor framing notes
  - This is a clean bridge into Topic 3 Research Methods

## Lab 7: Micro-history lessons with scavenger hunts (Cummings Center 5 Minute History Lessons)
- Hub
  - https://www.uakron.edu/chp/education/videos.dot
- Example teacher guide PDF
  - https://www.uakron.edu/chp/education/pdfs/Episode7_TeachersGuide_5MHL.pdf
- Activity type
  - Short video plus scavenger hunt and discussion prompts
- Output type
  - Completed scavenger hunt plus one synthesis claim
- Typical completion time
  - 15–25 minutes
- Submission evidence format
  - Completed scavenger hunt answers
  - One sentence linking the episode to a broader trend in psychology
- Instructor framing notes
  - Use these as “history in small doses” throughout the semester, not only in Topic 1

## Teaching designs and ideas

## Option A: One 50-minute “history as evidence” lab day
- Warm-up: Annenberg timeline scavenger hunt
- Main: One Akron online exhibit with its teaching resource
- Wrap: One method statement
  - “How did psychology try to make its claims credible in this era?”

## Option B: Station rotation (works in any classroom with devices)
- Station 1: Annenberg timeline
- Station 2: APA timeline
- Station 3: Akron online exhibit
- Station 4: Barnard apparatus analysis
- Station 5: Smithsonian Learning Lab collection

Grading pattern
- One screenshot per station
- One coded classification per station
- One limitation per station, such as “this artifact does not show X”

## Option C: Asynchronous lab (D2L-friendly)
- Students complete exactly one exhibit-based lab (Akron or Smithsonian)
- Students submit:
  - one artifact “source card”
  - one paragraph explaining how that artifact reflects a historical assumption in psychology

## Debrief prompts that force lab thinking
- What is the “independent variable” in the history story
  - changed methods, changed institutions, changed technology, changed social context
- What is the “dependent variable”
  - what psychologists measured, what counts as evidence, what gets published or funded
- What is one alternative explanation
  - social forces, war, migration policy, technology limits, academic incentives
- What is one ethical hazard
  - who gets harmed when a tool or theory is misapplied
---
```

```json
          "One ethics hazard note: a plausible misuse and a harm pathway"
        ],
        "instructor_framing_notes": [
          "Require evidence-based critique anchored in the item metadata or visible artifact information",
          "Require a non-causal phrasing check: avoid implying historical artifact equals direct proof of causal claims"
        ],
        "privacy_and_safety_notes": "Low risk. Content can involve sensitive historical material; provide a content warning and an alternate item set if needed.",
        "opt_out_available": true,
        "opt_out_description": "Analyze a provided instructor-selected item screenshot set instead of browsing the full collection."
      },
      {
        "lab_id": "HIST-06",
        "lab_title": "Artifact analysis lab (Barnard Psychology Virtual Museum)",
        "urls": ["https://psychology.barnard.edu/psych-psych-virtual-museum"],
        "activity_type": "artifact_browsing_and_structured_inference",
        "output_types": ["instrument_inference_worksheet"],
        "typical_completion_time_minutes": { "min": 15, "max": 25 },
        "submission_evidence_format": ["short_reflection", "categorical_output", "screenshot"],
        "submission_requirements": [
          "Choose one apparatus and answer four prompts: what it is; what it might measure; what assumption it embeds; what would count as evidence",
          "One bridge statement connecting the apparatus to Topic 3 Research Methods"
        ],
        "instructor_framing_notes": [
          "Require students to label assumptions explicitly (for example, behavior is measurable, introspection is valid evidence, reaction time indexes processing)",
          "Require one alternative interpretation for what the apparatus could be measuring"
        ],
        "privacy_and_safety_notes": "Low risk. No personal disclosure required.",
        "opt_out_available": false
      },
      {
        "lab_id": "HIST-07",
        "lab_title": "Micro-history lessons with scavenger hunts (Cummings Center 5 Minute History Lessons)",
        "urls": [
          "https://www.uakron.edu/chp/education/videos.dot",
          "https://www.uakron.edu/chp/education/pdfs/Episode7_TeachersGuide_5MHL.pdf"
        ],
        "activity_type": "short_video_plus_scavenger_hunt_prompts",
        "output_types": ["scavenger_hunt_answers", "one_sentence_synthesis_claim"],
        "typical_completion_time_minutes": { "min": 15, "max": 25 },
        "submission_evidence_format": ["short_reflection", "categorical_output"],
        "submission_requirements": [
          "Completed scavenger hunt answers from the guide prompts",
          "One sentence linking the episode to a broader trend in psychology"
        ],
        "instructor_framing_notes": [
          "Use as distributed history practice across the semester, not only in Topic 1",
          "Require a single trend label: methods shift, ethics shift, applied shift, institutional shift"
        ],
        "privacy_and_safety_notes": "Low risk. No personal disclosure required.",
        "opt_out_available": false
      }
    ],
    "teaching_designs_and_ideas": [
      {
        "design_id": "HIST-DESIGN-01",
        "design_name": "One 50-minute history-as-evidence lab day",
        "steps": [
          "Warm-up: Annenberg timeline scavenger hunt",
          "Main: One Akron online exhibit with its attached teaching resource prompts",
          "Wrap: One methods statement: how did psychology try to make its claims credible in this era"

        "grading_artifact": [
          "Two screenshots",
          "Two classification labels",
          "One paragraph methods statement"
        ]

        "design_id": "HIST-DESIGN-02",
        "design_name": "Station rotation (device-friendly)",

          "Annenberg timeline",

          "Smithsonian Learning Lab collection"

          "One screenshot per station",
          "One coded classification per station",
          "One limitation statement per station"

      },
```
